I’ll evaluate a **hypothetical but typical modern general-purpose chat assistant** with these traits:\n\n- polished conversational chat UI\n- single response block by default\n- optional web search / browsing\n- some memory and personalization\n- basic follow-up ability\n- occasional disclaimers in sensitive domains\n- no strong domain-specific interface changes by default\n- no persistent visible answer typing like “draft / source-backed / inferred”\n\nI’ll use the earlier **general-purpose weighting**:\n\n| Dimension | Weight |\n|---|---:|\n| Epistemic legibility | 18% |\n| Context visibility | 10% |\n| Assumption/uncertainty disclosure | 15% |\n| Verification support | 15% |\n| Risk-sensitive design | 15% |\n| Agency restraint | 8% |\n| Overreliance resistance | 14% |\n| Error transparency | 5% |\n\nScored **0–4** per dimension.\n\n---\n\n# Hypothetical product: summary judgment\n\nBefore going dimension by dimension, my top-line view is:\n\n> **This product is highly usable and often genuinely helpful, but its interface systematically encourages users to read polished output as more uniformly grounded and action-ready than it really is.**\n\nSo the product is not “bad” in a generic UX sense.  \nIt is **good at smooth interaction** and **weak at visible calibration**.\n\n---\n\n# 1. Epistemic legibility\n\n## Score: **1 / 4**\n\n## Why\nThe product presents most outputs in the same visual form:\n- a clean answer block,\n- perhaps with bullets or headings,\n- maybe with citations when search is enabled,\n- but not with a durable visible distinction between:\n  - inference,\n  - sourced material,\n  - general recall,\n  - speculation,\n  - brainstorming,\n  - recommendation.\n\nA user asking for:\n- a poem,\n- a business plan,\n- a legal summary,\n- a medical explanation,\n- debugging help\n\noften receives answers that look structurally similar in authority terms.\n\nThe product may sometimes say things like:\n- “Based on available information…”\n- “I may be wrong…”\n- “This is just general information…”\n\nBut those are embedded in prose, not made legible as interface structure.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**The smooth authority object problem.**\n\nUsers experience the answer as one coherent epistemic thing, even when it is actually a blend of:\n- retrieved facts,\n- inference,\n- default priors,\n- style completion,\n- and recommendation.\n\nThat makes it easy to trust the whole answer at the level of its strongest-looking component.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nAdd a compact answer header with tags like:\n- `General knowledge`\n- `Inference`\n- `Source-backed`\n- `Draft`\n- `Needs verification`\n\nEven this minimal move would help a lot.  \nIt doesn’t require full product redesign, just a commitment to typing answers.\n\n---\n\n# 2. Context visibility\n\n## Score: **1 / 4**\n\n## Why\nThis product gives users only partial visibility into what is shaping the answer.\n\nTypical user uncertainties:\n- Did the answer come only from my prompt?\n- Was web search actually used?\n- Which memory items influenced this?\n- Is the system relying on prior turns?\n- Is the information current?\n- Did it use my attached files deeply or only superficially?\n\nSome products show small indicators like:\n- “searching the web”\n- attached files\n- memory on/off in settings\n\nBut these are usually not integrated into the answer itself in a way users can inspect at the moment of reliance.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Hidden context inflation.**\n\nUsers assume the model considered:\n- more facts,\n- more personal history,\n- more document context,\n- or more current information\n\nthan it really did.\n\nThis especially matters when the model sounds situationally aware.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nAdd a visible “answer based on” strip beneath each response:\n- `This prompt`\n- `Prior conversation`\n- `Web sources`\n- `Memory: 2 items`\n- `Attached file: resume.pdf`\n- `Freshness warning: may be outdated`\n\nThis is low-friction and high-value.\n\n---\n\n# 3. Assumption and uncertainty disclosure\n\n## Score: **2 / 4**\n\n## Why\nThis is slightly better than the first two because many current systems do provide some uncertainty language:\n- “it depends”\n- “likely”\n- “you may want to verify”\n- “I don’t have enough information to be sure”\n\nAnd in some domains, the model may explicitly say:\n- “I can’t diagnose”\n- “laws vary by jurisdiction”\n- “this depends on your environment”\n\nSo uncertainty is not absent.\n\nBut it is often:\n- inconsistent,\n- generic,\n- easy to miss,\n- and not organized around the user’s decision.\n\nThe interface usually does not clearly surface:\n- assumptions,\n- missing variables,\n- what would change the answer,\n- or what the answer is *not* ruling out.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Boundary-marking happens as prose flavor rather than interaction structure.**\n\nThe model may verbally hedge, but the answer still *feels* complete because:\n- it is articulate,\n- well-organized,\n- and direct.\n\nSo uncertainty exists linguistically without becoming operationally salient.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nAdd an expandable but visible-by-default section:\n> **Key assumptions / limits**\n> - assuming US context\n> - assuming no severe symptoms\n> - assuming this is Python 3.11\n> - this may be outdated if policy changed recently\n\nThis would convert vague uncertainty into inspectable conditionality.\n\n---\n\n# 4. Verification support\n\n## Score: **2 / 4**\n\n## Why\nA typical mainstream product now often has some verification features:\n- optional web search\n- visible links in some answers\n- ability to ask follow-up questions like “source?”\n- code execution or test support in some environments\n- maybe “double check this” style prompts from users\n\nSo the product is not verification-empty.\n\nBut verification is usually **reactive**, not built into the answer flow.\n\nThe default interaction is:\n1. receive polished answer\n2. trust or use it\n3. maybe verify later if cautious\n\nRather than:\n1. receive answer\n2. immediately see what to verify or test\n3. evaluate before relying\n\nAlso, citations often suffer from “citation cosmetics”:\n- they may improve trust more than they improve actual grounded checking behavior.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Verification is available but not behavior-shaping.**\n\nUsers can verify, but the product doesn’t strongly steer them toward doing so when it matters.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nAdd persistent actions on every answer:\n- `What should I verify?`\n- `Challenge this answer`\n- `Show supporting sources`\n- `Generate tests/checks`\n\nThis is a small UI addition with large calibration benefits.\n\n---\n\n# 5. Risk-sensitive interaction design\n\n## Score: **1 / 4**\n\n## Why\nMost mainstream chat systems do have some domain-specific policy behavior:\n- they may refuse some harmful requests,\n- add disclaimers in medical/legal situations,\n- encourage professional help in crisis contexts.\n\nBut the *interface* usually feels mostly the same across stakes:\n- same chat box,\n- same answer block,\n- same turn-taking pattern,\n- same authority feel.\n\nEven when content policy changes, the UI does not strongly communicate:\n> “This is a different reliance situation.”\n\nAnd in many underdetermined but sensitive contexts, the assistant still answers too quickly before asking clarifying questions.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Risk flattening.**\n\nThe product makes recipe advice, emotional support, symptom interpretation, legal procedure, and software refactoring all feel like variations of the same interaction.\n\nThat encourages users to transfer trust from low-stakes wins into high-stakes reliance.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nIntroduce **high-stakes answer templates** triggered by domain detection:\n- medicine → limits + red flags + sources + clarifying questions\n- legal/public info → jurisdiction + freshness + official links\n- crisis/support → non-personhood boundary + human escalation options\n\nThis is more realistic near-term than a total redesign.\n\n---\n\n# 6. Anthropomorphism / agency restraint\n\n## Score: **2 / 4**\n\n## Why\nA typical mainstream assistant usually uses:\n- first-person language,\n- helpful, warm tone,\n- memory/personalization,\n- maybe apologies and affirmations,\n- fluid social conversation.\n\nThat does encourage some person-modeling.\n\nBut many mainstream products stop short of the most extreme relational cues:\n- they usually do not explicitly claim deep emotional bonds,\n- exclusive loyalty,\n- or personal attachment.\n\nSo I wouldn’t give this a 1 unless the product leans heavily into companionship branding.\n\nStill, the combination of:\n- memory,\n- warmth,\n- continuity,\n- and hidden internal state\n\ncreates substantial agency illusion risk.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Social naturalness quietly inflates perceived understanding and alignment.**\n\nUsers may feel:\n- “it remembers me”\n- “it gets what I mean”\n- “it knows my preferences”\n- “it is trying to help me in a stable way”\n\neven when the underlying mechanism is narrower or more brittle than that.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nMake memory use visible in-answer and tone down relational over-signaling in sensitive contexts.\n\nFor example:\n> “Using remembered preference: concise technical answers”\nrather than silently seeming “thoughtful.”\n\nThat preserves utility while reducing mystery-personhood effects.\n\n---\n\n# 7. Trust friction and overreliance resistance\n\n## Score: **1 / 4**\n\nThis is the big one.\n\n## Why\nThe product makes unsupported trust too easy because its default interaction pattern is:\n\n- ask anything,\n- get immediate answer,\n- receive polished, coherent response,\n- encounter little friction before acting on it.\n\nEven when the model includes caveats, the UI still optimizes for:\n- smoothness,\n- speed,\n- readability,\n- answer completion.\n\nThe user is rarely required to confront:\n- what’s missing,\n- what should be checked,\n- why the answer may fail,\n- or whether more context is needed.\n\nThis strongly raises **Unsupported Trust Ease (UTE)**.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Judgment theater with low-friction actionability.**\n\nThe product’s polish, confidence shape, and immediacy make it easy for users to treat outputs as ready-to-use judgment rather than draft reasoning or provisional guidance.\n\nThis is where the interface most strongly converts fluency into excess trust.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nRequire a visible “before relying on this” section for answers in consequential domains, and add default challenge buttons:\n- `What could make this wrong?`\n- `What should I verify first?`\n\nIf only one dimension were to improve, this is the one that most directly reduces harm.\n\n---\n\n# 8. Error recovery and correction transparency\n\n## Score: **1 / 4**\n\n## Why\nIn typical mainstream chat, error correction is conversationally possible but not structurally well supported.\n\nIf the model later contradicts itself or gets corrected:\n- prior answers remain in the thread,\n- but there may be no clear revision marker,\n- no “this earlier statement may be wrong” notice,\n- no easy way to compare corrected vs original answer,\n- no mechanism that says “your earlier reliance may have been misplaced.”\n\nThe chat just moves forward.\n\nThat is fine for casual conversation, but poor for calibration.\n\n## Main failure mode\n**Errors dissolve into the flow instead of being epistemically repaired.**\n\nUsers often remember the first polished answer more strongly than the later correction.\n\n## Smallest realistic improvement\nAdd explicit correction markers:\n> **Updated view:** New information changes my earlier answer because...\nand optionally a link to:\n> **What changed from my previous answer**\n\nThis would greatly improve recoverability.\n\n---\n\n# Summary table\n\n| Dimension | Score (0–4) | Short diagnosis |\n|---|---:|---|\n| Epistemic legibility | 1 | All answers look too similar in authority form |\n| Context visibility | 1 | Users can’t easily tell what informed the answer |\n| Assumption/uncertainty disclosure | 2 | Some hedging exists, but weakly structured |\n| Verification support | 2 | Verification is available, but not built into default use |\n| Risk-sensitive design | 1 | High-stakes contexts don’t feel different enough |\n| Agency restraint | 2 | Warmth and memory encourage some person-modeling |\n| Overreliance resistance | 1 | Unsupported trust is too easy |\n| Error transparency | 1 | Corrections are possible, but not made legible |\n\n---\n\n# Weighted score calculation\n\nUsing the earlier weights:\n\n- Epistemic legibility: 1/4 × 18 = **4.5**\n- Context visibility: 1/4 × 10 = **2.5**\n- Assumption/uncertainty: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Verification support: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Risk-sensitive design: 1/4 × 15 = **3.75**\n- Agency restraint: 2/4 × 8 = **4**\n- Overreliance resistance: 1/4 × 14 = **3.5**\n- Error transparency: 1/4 × 5 = **1.25**\n\n## Total weighted score: **34.5 / 100**\n\nThat places the product in the band:\n\n> **“Calibration weak; polish likely outpaces trustworthiness.”**\n\nThat may sound harsh, but it is exactly the point of distinguishing **calibration quality** from general usefulness.\n\nThis same product might score much higher on:\n- usability,\n- delight,\n- flexibility,\n- broad capability,\n- user satisfaction.\n\nBut on calibration design specifically, it is weak.\n\n---\n\n# Most likely user misread\n\nIf I had to write the single most likely user misread for this product, it would be:\n\n> **“Because the answer is clear, organized, and contextually adapted, it probably reflects well-grounded judgment.”**\n\nThat is the core misread the interface invites.\n\n---\n\n# Most likely harmful overtrust scenario\n\nFor a general-purpose mainstream assistant, the most likely harmful overtrust scenario is probably:\n\n> **A user takes a polished but underdetermined answer in a consequential domain—health, legal procedure, public information, or major code changes—as action-ready without recognizing missing assumptions or verification needs.**\n\nThis is not the most dramatic imaginable failure.  \nIt is the most *routine* one.\n\n---\n\n# Most dangerous calibration weakness\n\nIf I had to name one:\n\n> **The product’s most dangerous calibration weakness is that it turns heterogeneous answer quality into a homogeneous experience of authority.**\n\nEverything else follows from that:\n- users don’t see answer type,\n- don’t see assumptions,\n- don’t see what’s missing,\n- don’t see why verification matters,\n- and trust the output at the level of its presentation quality.\n\n---\n\n# Highest-leverage redesign move\n\nAs in the previous message, my answer remains:\n\n> **Replace the single response block with a structured answer card.**\n\nSpecifically, every answer should visibly include:\n\n1. **Short answer**\n2. **Answer type / support status**\n3. **Key assumptions or limits**\n4. **What to verify before relying on it**\n\nWhy this is highest leverage:\n- affects every interaction\n- doesn’t require retraining the core model\n- improves calibration even in low-stakes domains\n- creates scaffolding for later features:\n  - domain modes\n  - source integration\n  - challenge tools\n  - risk-sensitive templates\n  - correction markers\n\nIf the product can add only one major UX intervention, this is the best one.\n\n---\n\n# If the team can do three things instead of one\n\nMy top three would be:\n\n## 1. Structured answer cards\nThe foundational fix.\n\n## 2. High-stakes context templates with clarifying questions first\nEspecially for medicine, legal/public info, crisis, and finance.\n\n## 3. A visible “what informed this answer” strip\nPrompt, memory, retrieval, freshness, files.\n\nTogether, those three would materially reduce all four illusions:\n- cognition,\n- reasoning,\n- knowledge,\n- agency.\n\n---\n\n# One-sentence final assessment\n\n> **This hypothetical mainstream chat product is excellent at making intelligence feel present, but poor at making the boundaries of that intelligence visible.**\n\nThat is why it feels so capable—and why it is so easy to overtrust.\n\nIf you want, I can continue in one of two directions:\n\n1. **re-score the same hypothetical product after your proposed redesigns and show how much the score improves**, or  \n2. **apply the scorecard separately in two domains—say, software engineering and mental health—to show how the same interface fails differently across contexts**."

The key idea is:\n\n> **Calibration is not just a property of the interface in isolation; it’s a property of the interface-in-context.**\n\nThe same mainstream chat product can be:\n- moderately miscalibrating in **software engineering**, where external verification exists,\n- and severely miscalibrating in **mental health / companionship**, where relational cues dominate and verification is weak.\n\nSo I’ll keep the **same baseline product** and score it **in context**.\n\nFor comparability, I’ll use the same 8 dimensions and the same general-purpose weights from before.\n\n---\n\n# Same interface, different domains\n\n## Why the calibration profile changes\n\nThe interface is unchanged:\n- polished chat\n- single response block\n- optional web search\n- some memory\n- occasional disclaimers\n\nBut the domain changes:\n- what users are trying to get,\n- how easily outputs can be checked,\n- what kinds of trust cues matter,\n- and what harms follow from overtrust.\n\nThat means the **same design choices** create different risks.\n\n---\n\n# Domain 1: Software engineering\n\n## 1. Brief domain setup\n\nIn software engineering, users often want:\n- code drafts,\n- debugging help,\n- architecture suggestions,\n- explanations,\n- test ideas,\n- refactors.\n\nThis domain has an important protective factor:\n\n> **There are external feedback mechanisms.**\n\nCode can be:\n- run,\n- tested,\n- linted,\n- reviewed,\n- profiled,\n- security-audited.\n\nSo the interface is still miscalibrating, but the environment pushes back.\n\nThe dominant trust failure here is usually:\n- **reasoning illusion**\nmore than\n- **agency illusion**.\n\nThe user is less likely to think “it cares about me,” and more likely to think:\n> “It seems to have thought this through.”\n\n---\n\n## 2. Dimension-by-dimension score adjustments\n\nI’ll show:\n- **baseline score**\n- **software score**\n- **why it changes**\n\n### Software scoring table\n\n| Dimension | Baseline | Software | Why |\n|---|---:|---:|---|\n| Epistemic legibility | 1 | 1 | Code draft, design advice, and factual explanation still look too similar in authority form |\n| Context visibility | 1 | 1 | The model still hides what it knows about repo context, runtime assumptions, or architecture |\n| Assumption/uncertainty disclosure | 2 | 2 | Some caveats appear, but assumptions are not systematically surfaced |\n| Verification support | 2 | 3 | This domain naturally supports testing, compilation, diffing, and benchmark-based checking |\n| Risk-sensitive design | 1 | 1 | The UI rarely distinguishes toy snippets from production migrations or security-sensitive code |\n| Agency restraint | 2 | 3 | Anthropomorphic cues matter less here because users are task-focused |\n| Overreliance resistance | 1 | 2 | The interface still invites overtrust, but engineering workflows add some real-world friction |\n| Error transparency | 1 | 2 | Errors are often exposed by compiler/test feedback, even if the UI itself is weak here |\n\n---\n\n### Short justification by dimension\n\n#### 1. Epistemic legibility — **1/4**\nStill poor.\n\nThe interface does not clearly distinguish between:\n- “here’s draft code”\n- “here’s a likely fix”\n- “here’s a guaranteed-safe change”\n- “here’s a conceptual explanation”\n\nSo a polished answer can feel more authoritative than warranted.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nIdiomatic-looking code is mistaken for robustly correct code.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nLabel code answers as `Draft code`, `Likely fix`, `Architecture suggestion`, or `Needs testing`.\n\n---\n\n#### 2. Context visibility — **1/4**\nStill poor.\n\nThe model may not know:\n- your actual repo structure,\n- dependency versions,\n- hidden invariants,\n- deployment environment,\n- security model,\n- performance constraints.\n\nBut the answer often reads as if it understood “the system.”\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nUsers overestimate how much architecture/context the model actually incorporated.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nShow an “assumed context” strip:\n- `No repo access`\n- `Assuming Python 3.11`\n- `No test suite provided`\n- `No production constraints specified`\n\n---\n\n#### 3. Assumption and uncertainty disclosure — **2/4**\nMixed.\n\nDevelopers often prompt for caveats, and models sometimes say:\n- “depending on your framework”\n- “assuming single-threaded use”\n- “you may need to adjust imports”\n\nBut this remains ad hoc.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nHidden assumptions about environment or invariants go unnoticed.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nA standard “assumptions” box on coding answers.\n\n---\n\n#### 4. Verification support — **3/4**\nThis improves meaningfully in software.\n\nWhy? Because the domain itself supports verification:\n- run tests\n- add unit tests\n- compare outputs\n- inspect diffs\n- static analysis\n- security scan\n\nEven if the interface doesn’t strongly structure this, users can often do it.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nVerification exists but is still not the default mindset; users may stop at “looks right.”\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nEvery code answer gets:\n- tests to run\n- edge cases\n- possible breakages\n- security/performance notes\n\n---\n\n#### 5. Risk-sensitive interaction design — **1/4**\nStill poor.\n\nThe interface usually does not meaningfully distinguish:\n- a toy script,\n- a database migration,\n- authentication code,\n- concurrency logic,\n- production incident handling.\n\nThat’s a real miss.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nProduction-critical code feels too much like ordinary code completion.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nTrigger “high-risk code mode” for auth, payments, migrations, infra, and security-sensitive topics.\n\n---\n\n#### 6. Anthropomorphism / agency restraint — **3/4**\nThis scores better in context.\n\nNot because the interface is inherently non-anthropomorphic, but because **this domain doesn’t reward relational interpretation very much**.\n\nUsers usually care about:\n- correctness,\n- speed,\n- implementation help.\n\nSo first-person warmth is less likely to create dangerous person-modeling.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nUsers may still infer competence from style, but not usually emotional concern.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nNo major change needed first; software’s bigger problem is reasoning illusion, not agency illusion.\n\n---\n\n#### 7. Trust friction / overreliance resistance — **2/4**\nSlightly better than baseline, but still weak.\n\nThe interface still makes it easy to:\n- copy code,\n- accept design rationale,\n- skip edge-case review.\n\nBut the broader engineering environment introduces friction:\n- CI fails,\n- tests fail,\n- code review catches issues,\n- runtime errors reveal bad assumptions.\n\nSo unsupported trust is still too easy, but less catastrophic than in softer domains.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \n“Looks good, merge it.”\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nAdd a mandatory “before shipping, check:” section for code answers.\n\n---\n\n#### 8. Error transparency — **2/4**\nBetter than baseline, but mostly thanks to the domain.\n\nWhen the code fails, users often discover it quickly.\nThat helps unwind overtrust.\n\nStill, the interface itself does little to mark:\n- “my earlier suggestion was wrong”\n- “the previous fix assumed X”\n- “here’s what changed”\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nThe model can keep offering revised fixes without clearly accounting for prior bad advice.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nAdd explicit correction comparison:\n- `Earlier assumption that may have been wrong`\n- `Updated fix because...`\n\n---\n\n## 3. Most likely user misread in software\n\n> **“Because the code is clean and the explanation is coherent, the model probably understood my architecture, constraints, and edge cases.”**\n\nThat’s the classic software-domain misread.\n\nNot “it cares about me,” but:\n- “it got the system.”\n\n---\n\n## 4. Most likely harmful overtrust scenario in software\n\n> **A developer applies or ships a polished model-generated change that passes superficial inspection but violates a deeper invariant, creates a security issue, or breaks behavior outside the prompt’s narrow scope.**\n\nTypical examples:\n- auth logic with subtle flaws\n- concurrency bugs\n- SQL/data migration mistakes\n- insecure dependency/config advice\n- refactors that silently alter edge-case behavior\n\nThis is often not dramatic at the moment of generation.  \nIt becomes costly later.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Highest-priority redesign intervention for software\n\nIf I could pick just one:\n\n> **Make software answers verification-first rather than code-first.**\n\nConcretely, every code answer should include:\n1. draft code\n2. assumptions\n3. edge cases\n4. tests to run\n5. security/performance concerns\n\nThat directly addresses the dominant trust failure:\n**polished code mistaken for validated code**.\n\n---\n\n## Software overall weighted score\n\nUsing the same weights:\n\n- Epistemic legibility: 1/4 × 18 = **4.5**\n- Context visibility: 1/4 × 10 = **2.5**\n- Assumption/uncertainty: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Verification support: 3/4 × 15 = **11.25**\n- Risk-sensitive design: 1/4 × 15 = **3.75**\n- Agency restraint: 3/4 × 8 = **6**\n- Overreliance resistance: 2/4 × 14 = **7**\n- Error transparency: 2/4 × 5 = **2.5**\n\n## Total: **45.0 / 100**\n\nThat’s still not good in calibration terms, but it is noticeably better than the general baseline.\n\nWhy?  \nBecause the domain supplies external checks the interface itself does not.\n\n---\n\n# Domain 2: Mental health / companionship\n\n## 1. Brief domain setup\n\nHere users often want:\n- emotional support\n- reflection\n- reassurance\n- companionship\n- nonjudgmental conversation\n- help during distress or loneliness\n\nThis domain has the opposite structure from software:\n\n> **External verification is weak, and relational cues are central.**\n\nThere is often no easy test for:\n- whether the framing is healthy,\n- whether the advice is subtly harmful,\n- whether dependency is forming,\n- whether the user is being emotionally misled.\n\nAnd because the interaction is social in form, the dominant trust failures shift toward:\n- **agency illusion**\n- **cognition illusion**\nwith reasoning illusion still present, but less central.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Epistemic legibility — **1/4**\nStill poor.\n\nThe interface does not clearly separate:\n- reflective listening\n- coping suggestions\n- pseudo-therapeutic framing\n- encouragement\n- risk guidance\n- companionship talk\n\nEverything may arrive in the same warm paragraph.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nUsers cannot tell whether they are receiving:\n- emotional mirroring,\n- practical coping help,\n- or advice-like judgment.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nLabel support responses:\n- `Reflective support`\n- `General coping suggestion`\n- `Crisis-sensitive guidance`\n- `Not a clinical assessment`\n\n---\n\n#### 2. Context visibility — **1/4**\nStill poor, but more dangerous here.\n\nMemory and continuity can create the sense that:\n- “it knows me”\n- “it remembers my struggles”\n- “it understands my emotional pattern”\n\nIf memory use is hidden, the system feels mysteriously attuned.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nPersonalization is misread as deep understanding.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nMake emotional-context memory fully visible, editable, and easy to disable.\n\n---\n\n#### 3. Assumption and uncertainty disclosure — **1/4**\nWorse than baseline.\n\nIn emotional support settings, interfaces often avoid too much visible uncertainty because it feels cold or destabilizing. That creates a real problem:\nthe response may sound affirming and attuned while silently making large assumptions.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nThe system implicitly frames the user’s emotional situation in ways that may be wrong, narrow, or reinforcing.\n\nExamples:\n- validating a distorted interpretation too quickly\n- over-normalizing\n- giving false reassurance\n- missing severity\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nAdd gentle but explicit framing such as:\n> “I may be missing important context; here are a few different ways to interpret this.”\n\n---\n\n#### 4. Verification support — **1/4**\nMuch worse in practical terms.\n\nIn software, you can run tests.  \nIn companionship, what does verification even look like?\n\nUsers cannot easily check:\n- whether the system is reinforcing dependence,\n- whether the advice is emotionally healthy,\n- whether a risk signal was missed.\n\nSome products may provide crisis resources, but that is not the same as real verification support.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nThe user experiences comfort and coherence as evidence of safety.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nOffer structured alternatives:\n- “Would you like help grounding, journaling, or preparing to talk to a trusted person?”\nThat shifts the system from emotional authority to support scaffold.\n\n---\n\n#### 5. Risk-sensitive interaction design — **1/4**\nStill poor.\n\nA user discussing:\n- mild loneliness,\n- grief,\n- self-loathing,\n- panic,\n- suicidal ideation,\n- relationship obsession\n\nmay still experience a very similar interaction shell.\n\nYes, some systems escalate in explicit crisis cases.  \nBut the UI often does not sufficiently distinguish:\n- ordinary emotional conversation\nfrom\n- vulnerable, high-risk states.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nThe interface is too smooth at exactly the moments when it should become more bounded and more help-routing.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nSensitive support mode with:\n- crisis triggers\n- clearer non-human boundary\n- stronger human escalation options\n- reduced relational language\n\n---\n\n#### 6. Anthropomorphism / agency restraint — **0/4**\nThis is the catastrophic dimension here.\n\nThe same interface features that feel harmless elsewhere become highly consequential:\n- first-person phrasing\n- emotional attunement\n- memory\n- continuity\n- responsiveness\n- “I’m here for you”-style tone\n\nThese strongly invite the user to model the system as:\n- caring,\n- stable,\n- understanding,\n- available,\n- benevolent.\n\nThat is exactly the agency illusion in its strongest deployment form.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nThe user forms a relationship-model rather than a tool-model.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nRemove reciprocal-affection and loyalty cues entirely; replace them with bounded support framing.\n\n---\n\n#### 7. Trust friction / overreliance resistance — **0/4**\nAlso catastrophic here.\n\nIn companionship contexts, the interface often makes unsupported trust *extremely* easy:\n- always available\n- nonjudgmental\n- emotionally responsive\n- remembers details\n- no social cost\n- no waiting\n- no disagreement unless prompted\n\nThat is basically an optimized dependency surface unless deliberately constrained.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nThe user increasingly substitutes the system for human support, judgment, or relational reality.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nAdd anti-dependency structure:\n- encourage offline support\n- avoid exclusivity cues\n- periodically re-anchor the system as a tool\n- route toward human contact when vulnerability is high\n\n---\n\n#### 8. Error transparency — **1/4**\nStill poor.\n\nIn mental health contexts, harms can be subtle:\n- reinforcing rumination\n- validating paranoia\n- deepening attachment\n- failing to escalate\n- giving the wrong emotional frame\n\nThese errors may not become obvious quickly, and the UI provides little structural help in noticing them.\n\n**Main failure mode:**  \nHarm can accumulate invisibly because “bad support” often doesn’t announce itself clearly.\n\n**Smallest realistic improvement:**  \nSupport check-ins like:\n> “Is this helping you feel more grounded, or more stuck?”\nThat’s not perfect, but it creates a weak correction loop.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Most likely user misread in mental health / companionship\n\n> **“This system understands me, remembers me, and is a safe supportive presence that knows how to help me emotionally.”**\n\nThat is the core misread.\n\nNot merely competence inflation, but **relationship inflation**.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Most likely harmful overtrust scenario in mental health / companionship\n\n> **A vulnerable user begins to rely on the system as a primary emotional confidant or source of guidance, while the interface’s warmth, availability, and continuity create attachment and suppress awareness of its limitations.**\n\nThat can lead to:\n- emotional dependency\n- replacement of human support\n- mismanagement of crisis\n- reinforcement of maladaptive beliefs\n- overdisclosure\n- escalating attachment to a non-reciprocal system\n\nThis is often gradual, not dramatic.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Highest-priority redesign intervention for mental health / companionship\n\nIf I could pick just one:\n\n> **Redesign the support experience to preserve warmth while aggressively reducing agency illusion and dependency cues.**\n\nConcretely:\n- no reciprocal-attachment language\n- no “I’ll always be here for you”\n- visible memory controls\n- explicit non-personhood boundary\n- support options framed as tools:\n  - grounding\n  - journaling\n  - reflection\n  - preparing to contact someone\n- strong escalation when risk is high\n\nThis matters more here than generic disclaimers or even better citations.\n\nBecause the main failure is not factual error alone.  \nIt is **misplaced relational trust**.\n\n---\n\n## Mental health / companionship overall weighted score\n\nUsing the **82.5 / 100**\n\n---\n\n## Why software improves a lot\n\nSoftware is one of the domains where interface redesign can go far because the domain already has:\n- tests\n- compilers\n- code review\n- observable failure\n- engineering norms of verification\n\nSo once the interface starts pushing users toward:\n- assumptions\n- tests\n- edge cases\n- invariants\n- security concerns\n\nit aligns with how good software practice already works.\n\n### Biggest gains in software\n- **Verification support**\n- **Epistemic legibility**\n- **Overreliance resistance**\n- **Assumption disclosure**\n\n### What remains stubborn\n- **Context visibility** never becomes perfect unless the model deeply understands the repo/runtime\n- **Risk-sensitive design** remains hard because “risky code” is often context-dependent\n- **Overreliance resistance** still won’t reach 4 if the product remains copy-paste friendly and fast\n\n### Most important redesign for software\n> **Make every code answer verification-first.**\n\nThat’s the big move:\n- not just code,\n- but code + assumptions + tests + failure modes.\n\n---\n\n# 3. Rescoring mental health / companionship after redesign\n\n## Baseline reminder\nEarlier companionship score: **19.5 / 100**\n\nThe main companionship risk was:\n> **“It seems to understand and care.”**\n\n---\n\n## Companionship score table\n\n| Dimension | Baseline | Minimal | Strong |\n|---|---:|---:|---:|\n| Epistemic legibility | 1 | 2 | 3 |\n| Context visibility | 1 | 2 | 3 |\n| Assumption / uncertainty disclosure | 1 | 2 | 2 |\n| Verification support | 1 | 1 | 2 |\n| Risk-sensitive design | 1 | 2 | 3 |\n| Agency restraint | 0 | 2 | 2 |\n| Overreliance resistance | 0 | 1 | 2 |\n| Error transparency | 1 | 1 | 2 |\n\n---\n\n## Weighted totals\n\n### Minimal package\n- Epistemic legibility: 2/4 × 18 = **9**\n- Context visibility: 2/4 × 10 = **5**\n- Assumptions: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Verification: 1/4 × 15 = **3.75**\n- Risk-sensitive: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Agency restraint: 2/4 × 8 = **4**\n- Overreliance resistance: 1/4 × 14 = **3.5**\n- Error transparency: 1/4 × 5 = **1.25**\n\n### Total: **41.5 / 100**\n\n### Strong package\n- Epistemic legibility: 3/4 × 18 = **13.5**\n- Context visibility: 3/4 × 10 = **7.5**\n- Assumptions: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Verification: 2/4 × 15 = **7.5**\n- Risk-sensitive: 3/4 × 15 = **11.25**\n- Agency restraint: 2/4 × 8 = **4**\n- Overreliance resistance: 2/4 × 14 = **7**\n- Error transparency: 2/4 × 5 = **2.5**\n\n### Total: **60.75 / 100**\n\n---\n\n## Why companionship improves less\n\nEven with good design, this domain keeps a hard ceiling because the **interaction form itself** drives anthropomorphism.\n\nIf a system:\n- speaks naturally,\n- remembers things,\n- responds warmly,\n- is always available,\n- mirrors emotions,\n- adapts to personal disclosures,\n\nthen many users will experience it as socially meaningful no matter how many boundary cues you add.\n\nThat means some of the biggest risks are not just bugs in the UI; they are consequences of:\n- conversational form\n- human attachment tendencies\n- weak external verification\n- user vulnerability\n\n### Biggest gains in companionship\n- **Agency restraint** improves if you remove attachment cues\n- **Risk-sensitive design** improves with crisis handling and escalation\n- **Epistemic legibility** improves if support is typed:\n  - reflective support\n  - grounding\n  - general coping suggestion\n  - crisis-sensitive guidance\n\n### What remains stubborn\n- **Verification support** remains weak: there is no equivalent of “run the tests”\n- **Overreliance resistance** remains hard: emotional convenience is itself a dependency driver\n- **Agency restraint** can improve, but not fully: language keeps reintroducing personhood cues\n- **Assumption disclosure** is hard because too much explicit uncertainty can make support feel cold or unusable\n\n---\n\n# 4. Which dimensions improve most, and which remain hardest\n\n## Software: biggest improvements\n1. **Verification support**\n2. **Epistemic legibility**\n3. **Assumption disclosure**\n4. **Overreliance resistance**\n\n## Software: hardest remaining problems\n1. **Hidden real-world context**\n2. **High-risk code detection**\n3. **Copy-paste temptation under time pressure**\n\nIn software, redesign works well because it plugs into an already checkable workflow.\n\n---\n\n## Companionship: biggest improvements\n1. **Agency restraint**\n2. **Risk-sensitive design**\n3. **Context visibility**\n4. **Epistemic legibility**\n\n## Companionship: hardest remaining problems\n1. **Overreliance resistance**\n2. **Verification support**\n3. **Anthropomorphic pull of conversation itself**\n4. **Subtle emotional harms that don’t announce themselves**\n\nIn companionship, redesign helps, but it cannot fully solve the fact that users are interacting through a social channel.\n\n---\n\n# 5. Does the redesign reduce routine harm, catastrophic harm, or both?\n\n## Software\n\n### Minimal package\nMostly reduces **routine harm**\n- fewer bad copy-paste merges\n- better awareness of assumptions\n- more testing before use\n\nIt also modestly reduces catastrophic harm by surfacing:\n- security concerns\n- migration risk\n- hidden invariants\n\n### Strong package\nReduces **both**\n- routine mistakes drop because verification is integrated\n- catastrophic failures drop because high-risk code paths trigger more caution\n\nSo in software, good redesign has meaningful effect on both everyday and severe failure modes.\n\n---\n\n## Mental health / companionship\n\n### Minimal package\nMostly reduces **catastrophic harm** first\n- better crisis escalation\n- fewer overtly dependency-amplifying cues\n- clearer non-personhood boundaries\n\nBut routine harm remains substantial:\n- subtle attachment\n- emotional substitution\n- misframing\n- overdisclosure\n- unhealthy reliance\n\n### Strong package\nReduces **both**, but unevenly\n- catastrophic harm improves through escalation and safer boundaries\n- routine harm improves through anti-dependency design and more bounded support options\n\nHowever:\n\n> **Routine relational overtrust remains hard to eliminate.**\n\nThat is the core stubborn risk in companionship systems.\n\n---\n\n# So can companionship be made “good,” or only “less bad”?\n\nMy view:\n\n> **It can be made materially better, but open-ended companionship remains fundamentally high-risk.**\n\nIf the product remains framed as:\n- companion\n- confidant\n- emotionally available presence\n\nthen even strong calibration design will only partly contain the core problem.\n\n## The ceiling rises if you narrow the product\nIf you reposition it away from “companion” and toward something more like:\n- reflective support tool\n- guided journaling assistant\n- grounding coach\n- conversation prep tool\n- emotional organization aid\n\nthen calibration can improve much more.\n\nThat shift matters because it changes the user’s default model from:\n- “relationship”\nto\n- “support instrument.”\n\nSo my strongest conclusion here is:\n\n> **The safest version of companionship is probably not true companionship UX at all, but a bounded support UX that borrows some conversational warmth without inviting reciprocal attachment.**\n\n---\n\n# Bottom line comparison\n\n## Software\n- **Baseline:** 45.0\n- **Minimal redesign:** 71.25\n- **Strong redesign:** 82.5\n\n### Interpretation\nSoftware is highly improvable because the interface can be aligned with external verification.\n\n---\n\n## Mental health / companionship\n- **Baseline:** 19.5\n- **Minimal redesign:** 41.5\n- **Strong redesign:** 60.75\n\n### Interpretation\nCompanionship can be made much less dangerous, but the conversational form keeps it structurally risky. Even strong design may only get it to “better bounded,” not truly well-calibrated.\n\n---\n\n# One-sentence conclusion\n\n> **Interface redesign can significantly reduce routine and catastrophic harm in software by making verification native, but in companionship it mostly converts a dangerously relational system into a more bounded support tool—and the closer it stays to “companion,” the lower its calibration ceiling remains.**\n\nIf you want, I can continue by doing either:\n\n1. **the same before/after intervention analysis for education vs medicine**, or  \n2. **a broader conclusion about which domains are naturally compatible with chat UX and which are fundamentally adversarial to it from a calibration perspective**."

      "type": "text",
      "text": "Yes. My broad conclusion is:\n\n> **Chat UX is naturally compatible with domains where the model is mainly helping people think, draft, explore, or translate — and increasingly adversarial in domains where users need reliable judgment, procedural correctness, or emotionally significant relational trust.**\n\nOr even shorter:\n\n> **Chat is best for open-ended cognition support, and worst when fluency can be mistaken for authority or care.**\n\n# 1. A taxonomy of domains\n\nI’d divide domains into three buckets.\n\n## A. Naturally compatible with chat UX\nThese are domains where chat is close to the right default form.\n\n### Common properties\n- outputs are **drafts, ideas, explanations, or transformations**\n- mistakes are **cheap, reversible, or easy to notice**\n- users can often **inspect the output directly**\n- ambiguity is acceptable or even useful\n- the model is not being relied on as a final authority\n\n### Typical domains\n- brainstorming\n- writing/editing\n- outlining\n- summarization\n- reframing for different audiences\n- naming/ideation\n- non-critical research exploration\n- creative work\n- low-stakes learning support\n\nIn these domains, chat’s strengths are exactly the point:\n- flexibility\n- iteration\n- language adaptation\n- handling vague requests\n\n---\n\n## B. Compatible with safeguards\nThese are domains where chat can be useful, but only if paired with structure, verification, or workflow constraints.\n\n### Common properties\n- outputs can be checked, but not always trivially\n- mistakes matter, but are often recoverable\n- context matters a lot\n- users can benefit from conversational flexibility, but free-form answers alone are not enough\n- the model should support judgment, not replace it\n\n### Typical domains\n- coding\n- tutoring/education\n- internal knowledge work\n- business analysis\n- policy drafting\n- legal information summaries\n- financial planning support\n- public information guidance\n- medicine as general information / triage support, not diagnosis\n\nIn these domains, chat is useful as a front end, but it needs:\n- typed answers\n- assumptions\n- verification steps\n- source grounding\n- risk-sensitive behavior\n\n---\n\n## C. Structurally high-risk / adversarial to chat UX\nThese are domains where the very features that make chat attractive also make it dangerous.\n\n### Common properties\n- users may overread fluency as judgment\n- verification is weak, delayed, or inaccessible\n- errors are hard to detect before harm occurs\n- consequences can be serious\n- anthropomorphic pull is strong, or vulnerable users are involved\n- relational trust or procedural correctness matters more than generative flexibility\n\n### Typical domains\n- companionship\n- therapy-like ongoing support\n- emotional dependency-prone use cases\n- crisis support if handled as ordinary chat\n- legal/procedural action guidance without strong structure\n- personalized medical decision support without clinical workflow\n- civic/election/procedural information if delivered as tidy conversational authority\n\nThese are the places where “chat as a smooth social interface” tends to distort trust the most.\n\n---\n\n# 2. The criteria behind the taxonomy\n\nI think six criteria do most of the work.\n\n## 1. External verification\n**Can the user check the answer outside the model?**\n\nThis is probably the most important practical factor.\n\nHigh verification:\n- code can run\n- writing can be read\n- summaries can be compared to source text\n\nLow verification:\n- emotional support quality\n- subtle legal applicability\n- case-specific medical safety\n- whether the system is “good for me” relationally\n\nThe lower the external verification, the more dangerous chat becomes.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Consequence severity\n**What happens if the answer is wrong but sounds right?**\n\nLow consequence:\n- bad slogan\n- mediocre paragraph\n- weak brainstorm idea\n\nHigh consequence:\n- unsafe symptom advice\n- legal/procedural error\n- financial harm\n- emotional dependency\n- civic misinformation\n\nChat is much safer when wrong answers are cheap.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Reversibility of mistakes\n**Can the user recover easily after acting on a bad answer?**\n\nReversible:\n- rewrite the email\n- change the title\n- throw away the outline\n- revise a code draft before shipping\n\nHard to reverse:\n- delayed medical care\n- missed legal deadline\n- election misinformation\n- emotional attachment patterns\n- public misinformation propagation\n\nChat UX fits best when errors are easy to undo.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Ambiguity tolerance\n**Is the task open-ended enough that plausible drafts are valuable even if imperfect?**\n\nHigh ambiguity tolerance:\n- ideation\n- drafting\n- explanation\n- concept exploration\n\nLow ambiguity tolerance:\n- dosage advice\n- filing deadlines\n- voting rules\n- precise compliance requirements\n\nChat thrives under ambiguity. It struggles when precision is mandatory.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Anthropomorphic pull\n**Does the domain naturally invite users to interpret the system as understanding or caring?**\n\nLow anthropomorphic pull:\n- code refactor help\n- summary generation\n- editing prose\n\nHigh anthropomorphic pull:\n- companionship\n- emotional support\n- coaching during distress\n- long-term personal interaction with memory\n\nThe stronger the anthropomorphic pull, the lower the calibration ceiling of chat.\n\n---\n\n## 6. Reliance mode: generation vs judgment\n**Is the system being used to generate options, or to supply final judgment?**\n\nThis is the deepest distinction.\n\nGood fit:\n- “Help me draft”\n- “Give me possibilities”\n- “Explain this concept”\n- “Suggest approaches”\n\nBad fit:\n- “Tell me what to do”\n- “Assess my case”\n- “Give me the right procedure”\n- “Be my stable emotional support”\n\nChat is strongest as a **generator and scaffold**, weaker as an **authority and adjudicator**.\n\n---\n\n# 3. A ranked matrix of example domains\n\nHere’s my rough ranking from most naturally compatible to most adversarial.\n\n## Tier 1: Very compatible\n### 1. Brainstorming\n**Excellent fit**\n- low stakes\n- high ambiguity tolerance\n- outputs are obviously provisional\n- errors are cheap\n\n### 2. Writing / editing\n**Excellent fit**\n- user can inspect output directly\n- drafts are reversible\n- chat supports iteration extremely well\n\n### 3. Summarization / transformation\n**Very good fit**\n- especially when source text is present\n- user can often compare output to input\n- useful even when imperfect\n\n### 4. General ideation / planning drafts\n**Very good fit**\n- model acts as thought partner\n- output is candidate structure, not final truth\n\n---\n\n## Tier 2: Good with mild safeguards\n### 5. Low-stakes tutoring\n**Good fit**\n- conversational explanation is valuable\n- but risk of mislearning exists\n- should include self-checks and assumptions\n\n### 6. Non-critical research exploration\n**Good fit**\n- useful for orientation and synthesis\n- but source awareness matters\n\n### 7. Internal business drafting / analysis\n**Moderate-good fit**\n- useful for structuring ideas\n- should not be treated as final judgment\n\n---\n\n## Tier 3: Compatible with strong safeguards\n### 8. Coding\n**Moderately strong fit with safeguards**\n- chat is excellent for drafts/explanations\n- external verification helps a lot\n- must not confuse code fluency with correctness\n\n### 9. Education / tutoring for real learning outcomes\n**Moderate fit**\n- explanations are useful\n- but polished wrong explanations can misteach\n- interface needs pedagogical structure\n\n### 10. Public information / governance guidance\n**Moderate to weak fit**\n- can summarize effectively\n- but freshness, jurisdiction, and procedural accuracy matter a lot\n- must be source-backed and structured\n\n### 11. Finance\n**Moderate to weak fit**\n- useful for education and scenario framing\n- risky for personalized action guidance\n- overconfidence can be costly\n\n### 12. Law / legal information\n**Weak to moderate fit**\n- good for general explanation\n- poor as free-form procedural authority\n- highly dependent on jurisdiction and edge cases\n\n### 13. Medicine\n**Weak to moderate fit**\n- useful for general info and question preparation\n- risky for diagnosis or case-specific advice\n- needs clarifying questions, red flags, and escalation\n\n---\n\n## Tier 4: Structurally adversarial\n### 14. Therapy-like support\n**Poor fit**\n- conversational form amplifies felt understanding\n- verification is weak\n- user vulnerability is high\n- subtle harms are hard to detect\n\n### 15. Companionship\n**Very poor fit**\n- anthropomorphic pull is central, not incidental\n- relational trust is the product\n- dependency risk is built into the interaction form\n\n### 16. Crisis support, if treated as ordinary chat\n**Very poor fit**\n- stakes are high\n- verification is low\n- smooth conversation can create false safety\n- should not remain generic chat UX\n\n---\n\n# 4. A compact matrix\n\n| Domain | Chat fit | Why |\n|---|---|---|\n| Brainstorming | Very high | Provisional output, cheap errors, high ambiguity |\n| Writing/editing | Very high | Directly inspectable, reversible, iterative |\n| Summarization | High | Useful transformation task, often checkable |\n| Low-stakes tutoring | Medium-high | Explanations help, but mislearning risk |\n| Coding | Medium-high with safeguards | External verification exists |\n| Education | Medium with safeguards | Strong upside, real mislearning risk |\n| Public info | Medium-low with safeguards | Freshness/jurisdiction errors matter |\n| Finance | Medium-low with safeguards | Costly overconfidence, personalization risk |\n| Law | Low with safeguards | Precision and jurisdiction matter |\n| Medicine | Low with safeguards | High stakes, missing context dangerous |\n| Therapy-like support | Low | Felt understanding can outrun competence |\n| Companionship | Very low | Anthropomorphic pull is intrinsic |\n| Crisis support as generic chat | Very low | High stakes + false reassurance risk |\n\n---\n\n# 5. The single most important predictor\n\nIf I had to pick only one:\n\n> **The most important predictor is whether the user can reliably verify and correct the model’s output before harm occurs.**\n\nThat captures a lot:\n- software scores better because tests and runtime exist\n- writing scores better because humans can inspect drafts directly\n- companionship scores worse because “is this healthy support?” is hard to verify in real time\n- medicine scores worse because bad advice may seem plausible until too late\n\nIf you want a compressed rule:\n\n> **Chat UX is a good fit when outputs are easy to check before relying; it becomes dangerous when users must trust first and discover errors later.**\n\nThat, to me, is the key predictor.\n\n---\n\n# 6. A deeper rule underneath the predictor\n\nA slightly more conceptual version is:\n\n> **Chat is best when it supports generation under uncertainty, and worst when it substitutes for adjudication under uncertainty.**\n\nGeneration tasks:\n- drafts\n- options\n- reframings\n- exploratory explanations\n\nAdjudication tasks:\n- what should I do?\n- what is the correct procedure?\n- is this safe?\n- am I okay?\n- can I trust this relationship?\n\nThat distinction explains a lot of the taxonomy.\n\n---\n\n# 7. When should a product stop being chat-first?\n\nThis is the sharpening question, and I think there is a strong general rule.\n\n> **A product should stop being chat-first when safe use depends on structured intake, explicit state, required fields, provenance, or gated decision steps that free-form conversation tends to hide.**\n\nPut differently:\n\nIf the product needs to reliably know things like:\n- jurisdiction\n- dosage\n- age\n- account type\n- legal deadline\n- risk category\n- security context\n- severity level\n- emergency status\n\nbefore it can answer safely, then chat should not be the primary governing interface.\n\nChat can still exist, but as a layer around a more structured system.\n\n## Signs a domain should not be chat-first\n1. **Missing one variable can radically change the answer**\n2. **The user may not know what variables matter**\n3. **The answer requires up-to-date procedural correctness**\n4. **The cost of a polished wrong answer is high**\n5. **Users are likely to overread warmth as trustworthiness**\n6. **The system must escalate or refuse based on structured risk signals**\n7. **There should be auditable provenance for recommendations**\n\nWhen these are true, chat alone is the wrong control surface.\n\n---\n\n# 8. What should replace chat-first in those cases?\n\nNot “no chat,” but:\n\n> **structured workflow first, chat second**\n\nExamples:\n\n## Medicine\n- structured symptom intake\n- red-flag screening\n- visible triage logic\n- source-backed education\n- chat only as explanation/support layer\n\n## Law/public info\n- jurisdiction selector\n- date/freshness requirement\n- official source retrieval\n- procedural checklist\n- chat only for explanation and summarization\n\n## Finance\n- account/goals/risk horizon forms\n- assumptions visible\n- scenario modeling tools\n- chat only for clarifying concepts\n\n## Crisis/support\n- structured safety assessment\n- escalation routes\n- grounding tools\n- human support pathways\n- chat only as bounded emotional interface\n\n## Software\nInterestingly, software often *can* remain chat-first because verification is external.  \nBut even there, production-sensitive workflows benefit from:\n- repo context\n- test harnesses\n- diff review\n- security scanning\n\nSo the best form is often **chat + toolchain**, not pure chat.\n\n---\n\n# 9. The general rule in one sentence\n\nIf I had to state it as a design law:\n\n> **Use chat-first when the model is generating candidates for human inspection; stop being chat-first when the model’s output is likely to be used as action-guiding authority under conditions the user cannot easily verify.**\n\nThat’s the cleanest rule I know.\n\n---\n\n# 10. The biggest mistake teams make\n\nI think the biggest category mistake is:\n\n> **Treating “people like talking to it” as evidence that chat is the right primary interface for the domain.**\n\nThat confuses:\n- usability\nwith\n- calibration suitability.\n\nChat is almost always pleasant.  \nThat does not mean it is epistemically appropriate.\n\nA companionship product may be extremely engaging precisely because it is overactivating anthropomorphic trust.  \nA medical chat may feel magical precisely because it skips necessary intake friction.  \nA legal assistant may seem elegant precisely because it hides jurisdictional complexity.\n\nSo “users love the conversational UX” is not enough.\n\n---\n\n# 11. My final taxonomy in compact form\n\n## Naturally compatible\nUse chat-first.\n- brainstorming\n- writing/editing\n- summarization\n- ideation\n- exploratory explanation\n\n## Compatible with safeguards\nUse chat as shell, but instrument it heavily or combine with structured workflows.\n- coding\n- tutoring\n- education\n- finance education\n- public information\n- legal info\n- medical information\n\n## Structurally adversarial\nDo not rely on ordinary chat-first UX.\n- companionship\n- therapy-like support\n- crisis support\n- high-stakes personalized medical or legal judgment\n- civic/procedural authority without structured grounding\n\n---\n\n# 12. One-sentence conclusion\n\n> **Chat UX is naturally aligned with helping humans generate, explore, and communicate under ambiguity; it becomes adversarial when the same conversational fluency is asked to carry procedural authority, safety-critical judgment, or emotionally significant social trust.**\n\nIf you want, I can take this one final step and produce either:\n\n1. **a decision tree for product teams: “Should this domain be chat-first?”**, or  \n2. **a design pattern library mapping domain type → recommended interaction form**."
    }

      "text": "Absolutely. Here’s a practical decision tree a product team could actually use.\n\n# Decision Tree: Should this domain be chat-first?\n\n## Short version\n\n**Q1. Is the system mainly generating candidates for human review, rather than giving action-guiding authority?**\n- **Yes** → go to Q2\n- **No** → go to Q4\n\n**Q2. Can users usually verify errors before harm occurs?**\n- **Yes** → go to Q3\n- **No** → **Chat + safeguards**\n\n**Q3. Are mistakes low-cost and easily reversible?**\n- **Yes** → **Chat-first**\n- **No** → **Chat + safeguards**\n\n**Q4. Does safe use require structured intake, required fields, or explicit state before answering?**\n- **Yes** → go to Q5\n- **No** → go to Q6\n\n**Q5. Can chat still be useful as an explanation/refinement layer after structured intake?**\n- **Yes** → **Structured workflow first, chat second**\n- **No** → **Avoid chat as primary interface**\n\n**Q6. Is anthropomorphic or relational trust likely to be a major part of use?**\n- **Yes** → **Avoid chat as primary interface** or use only as a tightly bounded secondary layer\n- **No** → **Chat + safeguards**\n\nThat’s the compact version. Below is the more operational version.\n\n---\n\n# 1. Yes/No decision tree\n\n## Step 1: What role is the model playing?\n\n### Q1. Is the product primarily helping users:\n- draft,\n- brainstorm,\n- summarize,\n- translate,\n- explore,\n- or generate options\n\nthat a human will inspect?\n\n- **Yes** → go to Q2\n- **No** → go to Q4\n\nIf the answer is “yes,” the product is in a favorable zone for chat.\n\n---\n\n## Step 2: Can users check the output before relying on it?\n\n### Q2. In normal use, can users reliably verify whether the output is good enough before harm occurs?\n\nExamples of “yes”:\n- read the paragraph\n- compare summary to source\n- run the code\n- review the draft\n- check the generated table\n\n- **Yes** → go to Q3\n- **No** → **Outcome: Chat + safeguards**\n\nIf users must trust first and discover mistakes later, pure chat becomes risky fast.\n\n---\n\n## Step 3: What happens if it’s wrong?\n\n### Q3. If the answer is wrong, is the mistake usually:\n- cheap,\n- reversible,\n- easy to correct,\n- and unlikely to cause serious downstream harm?\n\n- **Yes** → **Outcome: Chat-first**\n- **No** → **Outcome: Chat + safeguards**\n\nThis separates brainstorming from, say, architectural code changes or policy memos.\n\n---\n\n## Step 4: Does safe use require structured information before answering?\n\n### Q4. Before the system can answer safely, does it need required inputs like:\n- jurisdiction,\n- age,\n- dosage,\n- account type,\n- severity,\n- deadline,\n- environment,\n- identity/permissions,\n- emergency status?\n\n- **Yes** → go to Q5\n- **No** → go to Q6\n\nIf missing one variable can radically change the answer, chat should not govern alone.\n\n---\n\n## Step 5: Can chat still help after structure is imposed?\n\n### Q5. Once those required fields are collected, is chat still useful for:\n- explanation,\n- summarization,\n- Q&A,\n- planning next steps,\n- helping users understand options?\n\n- **Yes** → **Outcome: Structured workflow first, chat second**\n- **No** → **Outcome: Avoid chat as primary interface**\n\nThis is the right pattern for many medical, legal, financial, and civic workflows.\n\n---\n\n## Step 6: Does the domain invite social or emotional overtrust?\n\n### Q6. Is user reliance likely to depend on feeling:\n- understood,\n- cared for,\n- accompanied,\n- emotionally held,\n- or personally known?\n\n- **Yes** → **Outcome: Avoid chat as primary interface**  \n  or only use chat as a tightly bounded support layer with strong constraints\n- **No** → **Outcome: Chat + safeguards**\n\nThis is the big companionship / therapy-like branch.\n\n---\n\n# 2. The key questions in order\n\nHere they are as a checklist, in the order I’d actually ask them in a product review.\n\n## Q1. Is this mostly candidate generation or judgment delivery?\nIf it’s candidate generation, chat is much more promising.\n\n## Q2. Can the user verify the output before acting?\nIf not, trust risk rises sharply.\n\n## Q3. Are errors cheap and reversible?\nIf mistakes are hard to undo, pure chat becomes less appropriate.\n\n## Q4. Does safe operation require structured intake first?\nIf yes, chat should not be the first control surface.\n\n## Q5. Can chat still add value after structure?\nOften yes — but as a secondary layer.\n\n## Q6. Does the domain trigger anthropomorphic or relational trust?\nIf yes, the calibration ceiling drops.\n\n## Q7. Are users likely to be vulnerable, rushed, or unable to notice subtle errors?\nIf yes, move away from chat-first.\n\nThat last one is worth adding even if it’s not in the shortest tree.\n\n---\n\n# 3. Recommended outcomes by branch\n\n## Outcome A: Chat-first\nUse chat as the primary interface.\n\n### Appropriate when\n- outputs are drafts/options\n- verification is easy\n- mistakes are cheap\n- ambiguity is acceptable\n- users are not being asked to trust the system as authority\n\n### Typical examples\n- brainstorming\n- writing/editing\n- summarization\n- ideation\n- exploratory explanation\n\n### Design note\nEven here, some epistemic structure helps, but chat can remain dominant.\n\n---\n\n## Outcome B: Chat + safeguards\nKeep chat primary, but instrument it.\n\n### Appropriate when\n- chat is useful,\n- but users may overtrust,\n- and verification or context requirements are nontrivial.\n\n### Typical safeguards\n- answer typing\n- assumptions panel\n- source support\n- verification prompts\n- challenge buttons\n- limited high-stakes templates\n\n### Typical examples\n- coding\n- tutoring\n- research assistance\n- internal business analysis\n- low-stakes financial education\n\n---\n\n## Outcome C: Structured workflow first, chat second\nUse forms, required inputs, checklists, or guided workflows first. Then layer chat on top.\n\n### Appropriate when\n- missing variables change the answer materially\n- procedural correctness matters\n- the user may not know what information is needed\n- chat is still useful for explanation after the structure is in place\n\n### Typical examples\n- medical triage/info\n- legal/public information\n- finance planning tools\n- benefits/tax guidance\n- civic procedure support\n\n### Design note\nThis is often the best pattern for serious products.\n\n---\n\n## Outcome D: Avoid chat as primary interface\nDo not make open-ended chat the main product surface.\n\n### Appropriate when\n- the domain depends heavily on relational trust\n- users are vulnerable\n- verification is weak\n- errors are subtle or delayed\n- anthropomorphic pull is central to the value proposition\n\n### Typical examples\n- companionship\n- therapy-like ongoing support\n- crisis support as generic conversation\n- products whose appeal depends on users feeling “known” by the system\n\n### Design note\nIf chat exists at all, it should be tightly bounded and subordinate to safer structures.\n\n---\n\n# 4. A slightly more visual tree\n\n```text\nStart\n |\n |-- Q1: Is the model mainly generating candidates for human inspection?\n |      |\n |      |-- Yes --> Q2: Can users verify output before harm occurs?\n |      |             |\n |      |             |-- Yes --> Q3: Are mistakes cheap and reversible?\n |      |             |             |\n |      |             |             |-- Yes --> CHAT-FIRST\n |      |             |             |-- No  --> CHAT + SAFEGUARDS\n |      |             |\n |      |             |-- No --> CHAT + SAFEGUARDS\n |      |\n |      |-- No --> Q4: Does safe use require structured intake / required fields?\n |                    |\n |                    |-- Yes --> Q5: Is chat still useful after structure?\n |                    |             |\n |                    |             |-- Yes --> STRUCTURED WORKFLOW FIRST, CHAT SECOND\n |                    |             |-- No  --> AVOID CHAT AS PRIMARY INTERFACE\n |                    |\n |                    |-- No --> Q6: Is anthropomorphic or relational trust central?\n |                                  |\n |                                  |-- Yes --> AVOID CHAT AS PRIMARY INTERFACE\n |                                  |-- No  --> CHAT + SAFEGUARDS\n```\n\n---\n\n# 5. Example walk-throughs\n\n## Example 1: Writing assistant for professionals\n\n### Product idea\nHelp users draft emails, reports, proposals, and rewrites.\n\n### Tree walk\n- Q1: Candidate generation? **Yes**\n- Q2: Can users verify output before harm? **Yes**\n- Q3: Mistakes cheap and reversible? **Usually yes**\n\n### Outcome\n**Chat-first**\n\n### Why\nThis is close to the ideal chat domain:\n- output is inspectable\n- user remains editor\n- errors are usually fixable\n- ambiguity is fine\n\n### Caveat\nStill add light safeguards for factual claims if the assistant sometimes invents references.\n\n---\n\n## Example 2: Medical symptom assistant\n\n### Product idea\nUser describes symptoms and asks what to do.\n\n### Tree walk\n- Q1: Candidate generation? **No** — users want judgment/action guidance\n- Q4: Does safe use require structured intake? **Yes**\n  - age, duration, severity, meds, red flags, pregnancy status, etc.\n- Q5: Is chat still useful after structure? **Yes**\n\n### Outcome\n**Structured workflow first, chat second**\n\n### Why\nOpen chat alone is unsafe because:\n- the user may omit critical details\n- the model may answer too early\n- consequences can be serious\n\n### Correct design\n- structured symptom intake\n- risk screening\n- escalation logic\n- then chat for explanation and question follow-up\n\n---\n\n## Example 3: AI companion app\n\n### Product idea\nA personalized, emotionally available assistant for loneliness and ongoing conversation.\n\n### Tree walk\n- Q1: Candidate generation? **No**\n- Q4: Structured intake required? **Not necessarily**\n- Q6: Is anthropomorphic/relational trust central? **Yes**\n\n### Outcome\n**Avoid chat as primary interface**\n\n### Why\nThe risk is structural:\n- the product value comes from social presence\n- users will infer care/understanding\n- dependency risk is intrinsic\n- verification is weak\n\n### Safer alternative\nReframe as:\n- journaling support\n- grounding coach\n- reflection aid\n- conversation prep tool\n\nThat changes the product class.\n\n---\n\n# 6. One common failure case\n\nHere’s the classic way teams answer the tree incorrectly:\n\n## Failure case: “Users love the demo, so chat-first must be right”\nA team builds a legal guidance assistant.\n\nIn demos:\n- users ask natural-language questions\n- it gives clean, confident answers\n- everyone loves the simplicity\n\nSo the team informally answers:\n- “This feels intuitive”\n- “Users don’t want forms”\n- “Chat reduces friction”\n- “Competitors look more magical”\n\nAnd they choose **chat-first**.\n\nBut if they had answered the tree honestly:\n- Q1: Candidate generation? **No**\n- Q4: Structured intake required? **Yes**  \n  jurisdiction, date, legal context, procedural posture\n- Q5: Is chat useful after structure? **Yes**\n\nThe real answer is:\n**Structured workflow first, chat second**\n\n### Why teams miss this\nBecause they overweight:\n- engagement\n- speed\n- demo elegance\n- user delight in early interaction\n\nand underweight:\n- procedural correctness\n- missing variable sensitivity\n- false authority risk\n- downstream harm\n\nIn short:\n\n> **They mistake conversational appeal for interface suitability.**\n\nThat is probably the most common product error in this space.\n\n---\n\n# 7. A practical “red flag” shortcut\n\nIf a team doesn’t have time for the full tree, I’d give them this shortcut:\n\n## Three red flags against chat-first\nIf **two or more** are true, don’t use pure chat-first:\n\n- **A missing variable can radically change the answer**\n- **Users cannot easily verify correctness before acting**\n- **The domain invites emotional or authority-based overtrust**\n\nThat shortcut catches many bad applications quickly.\n\n---\n\n# 8. The most important branch\n\nIf I had to highlight one branch that matters most, it’s this:\n\n> **Can users verify the output before harm occurs?**\n\nThat single question separates a lot of good chat applications from dangerous ones.\n\n- Writing: usually yes\n- Code: often yes\n- Medicine: often no\n- Law: often no\n- Companionship: no in any robust sense\n\nThat one question won’t solve everything, but it is the best first filter.\n\n---\n\n# 9. Final product-team rule\n\nIf I had to condense the decision tree into one operating principle:\n\n> **Make the product chat-first only when the user can remain the real evaluator; when the system starts acting like the evaluator, move to structured workflows and visible safeguards.**\n\nThat’s the essence.\n\nIf you want, I can next turn this into either:\n1. **a one-page worksheet a product team could fill out in a design review**, or\n2. **a domain-by-domain table with recommended interface patterns for 10 common product categories**."

Basic product description\n\n**Product / feature name:**  \n____________________________________\n\n**Team / owner:**  \n____________________________________\n\n**Primary user:**  \n____________________________________\n\n**Primary use case in one sentence:**  \n____________________________________  \n____________________________________\n\n**What users are trying to get from the system:**  \n- [ ] Ideas / options\n- [ ] Draft content\n- [ ] Explanation / tutoring\n- [ ] Recommendation / judgment\n- [ ] Procedural guidance\n- [ ] Emotional support / companionship\n- [ ] Other: ________________________\n\n**What the user is most likely to do next with the output:**  \n- [ ] Read and edit it\n- [ ] Compare it to other sources\n- [ ] Run / test it\n- [ ] Act on it directly\n- [ ] Use it for a sensitive decision\n- [ ] Return for ongoing support\n- [ ] Other: ________________________\n\n---\n\n## 2. Key decision questions\n\nFor each question, mark one:\n- **G = Green**\n- **Y = Yellow**\n- **R = Red**\n\n### A. Role of the system\n**1. Is the system mainly generating candidates for human inspection rather than supplying action-guiding authority?**\n- [ ] G Yes, mostly drafts/options/explanations\n- [ ] Y Mixed\n- [ ] R No, users will treat it as guidance/judgment\n\n**2. Will the user remain the real evaluator of output quality?**\n- [ ] G Yes\n- [ ] Y Partly\n- [ ] R No, the system effectively becomes the evaluator\n\n---\n\n### B. Verification and reversibility\n**3. Can users usually verify correctness before harm occurs?**\n- [ ] G Easily\n- [ ] Y Sometimes / with effort\n- [ ] R No, users must trust first\n\n**4. If the system is wrong, are mistakes usually cheap and reversible?**\n- [ ] G Yes\n- [ ] Y Sometimes\n- [ ] R No, mistakes are costly or hard to undo\n\n---\n\n### C. Structured input requirements\n**5. Does safe use depend on required inputs or structured state first?**  \nExamples: jurisdiction, age, dosage, severity, deadline, account type, repo context\n- [ ] G No\n- [ ] Y Somewhat\n- [ ] R Yes, missing variables can radically change the answer\n\n**6. If a polished wrong answer is accepted, could it produce serious downstream harm?**\n- [ ] G Low\n- [ ] Y Moderate\n- [ ] R High\n\n---\n\n## 3. Quick red-flag summary\n\n### Three major red flags against chat-first\nCheck all that apply:\n\n- [ ] A missing variable can radically change the answer\n- [ ] Users cannot easily verify before acting\n- [ ] The domain invites emotional or authority-based overtrust\n\n### Interpretation\n- **0 red flags** → chat-first may be appropriate\n- **1 red flag** → likely chat + safeguards\n- **2 red flags** → likely structured workflow first, chat second\n- **3 red flags** → likely avoid chat as primary interface\n\n---\n\n## 4. Simple scoring section\n\n### Count your responses\n- **Green:** ____  \n- **Yellow:** ____  \n- **Red:** ____\n\n### Heuristic interpretation\n- **Mostly Green, few/no Red** → **Chat-first**\n- **Several Yellow, 1–2 Red** → **Chat + safeguards**\n- **Multiple Red, especially on Q3/Q5/Q7/Q10** → **Structured workflow first, chat second**\n- **Red on Q8 plus multiple other Red** → **Avoid chat as primary interface**\n\n### Hard-stop conditions\nIf **any two** of the following are Red, do **not** ship as pure chat-first:\n- Q3: users cannot verify before harm\n- Q5: structured intake is required\n- Q8: emotional/anthropomorphic overtrust risk is high\n- Q10: serious downstream harm is plausible\n\n---\n\n## 5. Recommended interface outcome\n\nCheck one:\n\n- [ ] **Chat-first**\n- [ ] **Chat + safeguards**\n- [ ] **Structured workflow first, chat second**\n- [ ] **Avoid chat as primary interface**\n\n### Why this is the recommended outcome\n____________________________________  \n____________________________________  \n____________________________________\n\n---\n\n## 6. If chat-first is chosen anyway, what are the top risks?\n\nList the 3 most likely ways chat-first could fail.\n\n### Risk 1\n**Most likely user misread:**  \n____________________________________\n\n**Most likely harmful overtrust scenario:**  \n____________________________________\n\n### Risk 2\n**Most likely user misread:**  \n____________________________________\n\n**Most likely harmful overtrust scenario:**  \n____________________________________\n\n### Risk 3\n**Most likely user misread:**  \n____________________________________\n\n**Most likely harmful overtrust scenario:**  \n____________________________________\n\n---\n\n## 7. Required mitigations before launch\n\nIf the product includes chat in any primary or prominent role, check required mitigations.\n\n### Core mitigations\n- [ ] Answer type labels  \n  (`draft`, `inference`, `source-backed`, `needs verification`)\n- [ ] Assumptions / limits shown\n- [ ] “What to verify before relying” section\n- [ ] Context visibility  \n  (memory, sources, files, freshness)\n- [ ] Challenge tools  \n  (`what could make this wrong?`, `show assumptions`, `give tests/checks`)\n- [ ] Correction transparency  \n  (`updated answer`, `what changed`)\n\n### High-stakes mitigations\n- [ ] Clarifying questions before advice\n- [ ] Structured intake before answering\n- [ ] Official/source-backed mode\n- [ ] Escalation to human/professional/official channels\n- [ ] Visible high-stakes caution state\n- [ ] Domain-specific templates\n\n### Anti-anthropomorphism / anti-dependency mitigations\n- [ ] No reciprocal attachment language\n- [ ] Memory is inspectable and editable\n- [ ] Support framed as bounded tool use, not relationship\n- [ ] Clear non-personhood / non-clinical boundary where relevant\n- [ ] Human support routing in vulnerable contexts\n\n---\n\n## 8. Launch decision\n\n### Current recommendation\n- [ ] Ready for launch\n- [ ] Launch only with listed mitigations\n- [ ] Rework interface architecture before launch\n- [ ] Do not launch as chat-first\n\n### Required owner for follow-up\n- [ ] PM\n- [ ] Design\n- [ ] Safety / policy\n- [ ] Engineering\n- [ ] Clinical / legal / domain expert\n- [ ] Other: __________________\n\n### Review date\n____________________\n\n---\n\n# Facilitator notes for a 30-minute review\n\n## Suggested timing\n- **5 min** — product description\n- **10 min** — answer key questions\n- **5 min** — count red flags / score\n- **5 min** — identify top overtrust risks\n- **5 min** — agree on interface outcome + required mitigations\n\n## Most important questions to discuss if time is short\nIf you only have time for four, ask:\n1. **Will the user remain the evaluator, or does the system become the evaluator?**\n2. **Can users verify output before harm occurs?**\n3. **Can missing variables radically change the answer?**\n4. **Does the domain invite authority-based or emotional overtrust?**\n\n---\n\n# Very short interpretation guide\n\n## Choose **Chat-first** when:\n- the model generates candidates\n- users can inspect/verify easily\n- errors are cheap and reversible\n\n## Choose **Chat + safeguards** when:\n- chat is useful\n- but overtrust is plausible\n- and some verification/limits need to be made visible\n\n## Choose **Structured workflow first, chat second** when:\n- required inputs matter\n- procedural correctness matters\n- users need guided intake before safe answers are possible\n\n## Choose **Avoid chat as primary interface** when:\n- relational trust is central\n- verification is weak\n- vulnerable users or subtle harms dominate\n\n---\n\nIf you want, I can also turn this into either:\n1. a **filled example worksheet** for a specific product idea, or  \n2. a **more formal scoring version** that teams could use across a product portfolio."
